### (240) 7. Rossolimo 3...g6 and 4...bx - Rossolimo 6.Qxd4 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

Going for the Rossolimo! I've decided to pick this opening because it's giving us the following advantages: • We are avoiding a lot of complex theory • We are going to have a strategical battle (most Sicilian players are very gifted tactical players) • We are going to be better prepared than our opponent (most of the players are focusing on Open Sicilians, not on Anti-Sicilians) • We are still playing for an opening advantage, the Rossolimo is the main weapon of top players such as Fabiano Caruana, Magnus Carlsen, Levon Aronian, Anand Viswanathan, Michael Adams, and so on.</skip>

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

A move that has been popularized by the legendary Moldovian coach Viacheslav Chebanenko among his students: Viktor Bologan, Sergey Rublevsky, Viorel Iordachescu and others. At first, it looks insane to give up the Bishop in such a way without being provoked by Black, but White is playing for a better pawn structure. Of course, over the years, chess has moved on from such dogma that a Bishop is always better than a Knight, 'whatever the weather'.</skip>

**4...bxc6**

It's a riskier recapture strategically speaking because it creates an isolated pawn on a7, but on the other hand, it gives Black some dynamic assets, such as having a central pawn on the d-file, that will advance sooner or later. Black usually has two main plans: • To play it slow with: Bg7-d6-Nh6-f6-Nf7-O-O and say that the Bishop Pair is a strong asset when the position is going to open up • To play for a quick 5.-- ...d5 , trying to not give White an easy game</skip>

**5.d4**

I decided to recommend this move for the surprise value, plus I think it's an underrated line and people will play it a lot more in the future. White's play is fairly simple and effective, while in the other lines the play was generally double-edged. The point is that if Black plays the normal 5...cxd4 , we are going to reply by going 6.Qxd4 and ask a bunch of awkward questions. Black can try to keep our King in the center by going 5...Ba6!?, but as you will see White has enough resources to fight for an early edge.</skip>

**5...cxd4**

Has been played by Magnus Carlsen.</skip>

**6.Qxd4**

Forces Black to make an akward move.</skip>

**6...Nf6**

Is giving us an easy game.</skip>

**7.e5**

We have to question the Knight first.</skip>

**7...Nd5 8.O-O**

Getting our King to safety.</skip>

**8...Bg7 9.Qh4**

Preparing to go Bh6.</skip>

**9...O-O 10.c4**

getting rid of the Knight</skip>

**10...Nb4 11.Nc3**

the Knight is pointless on b4</skip>

### (241) 7. Rossolimo 3...g6 and 4...bx - Rossolimo 6.Qxd4 #2

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6**

**7...Nd5 8.O-O**

**8...Bg7 9.Qh4**

**9...O-O 10.c4**

**10...Nb6 11.Bh6 f6 12.Bxg7 Kxg7 13.c5**

fixing the weakness on d7</skip>

**13...Nd5 14.Nc3 Nxc3 15.bxc3**

White will place the Rooks in the center and press on Black's pawns.White can also do something like Qd4 and start pushing h4.</skip>

### (242) 7. Rossolimo 3...g6 and 4...bx - Rossolimo 6.Qxd4 #3

Overprotecting e5.</skip>

**12...Rb8 13.b3**

Defending the Queen-side and preparing to develop the Bishop.</skip>

**13...d5 14.cxd5**

We are creating a potential target on d5.</skip>

**14...cxd5 15.Be3**

White will play Rad1 next and Black can't even castle soon.</skip>

### (244) 7. Rossolimo 3...g6 and 4...bx - Rossolimo 6.Qxd4 #5

**10...Nc7 11.Nc3**

**11...Ne6 12.Re1**

**12...d6 13.b3**

Solidifying the Queen-side and preparing to play Bb2.</skip>

**13...Bb7 14.Bb2**

Developing the Bishop and preparing Rad1.</skip>

**14...O-O 15.Rad1**

Bringing the last piece into the game, and putting a lot of pressure on the d6 pawn.</skip>

**15...Re8 16.Ba1**

The Bishop is defended on a1.</skip>

Preparing to go h4 or Nh4.</skip>

Threatening to take on g6.</skip>

Eliminating an important pawn.</skip>

**19...Qc7 20.Rd3**

White got a nice attack on the King-side preparing to Rook lift next. 1-0 (47) Moza,M (2372)-Arnold,G (2253) ICCF email 2016</skip>

### (245) 7. Rossolimo 3...g6 and 4...bx - Rossolimo 6.Qxd4 #6 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6**

**5.d4**

**5...cxd4**

**6.Qxd4**

**6...Nf6**

**7.e5**

**7...Nd5 8.O-O**

**8...Bg7 9.Qh4**

**9...h6 10.c4**

**10...Nb4 11.Nc3**

Developing the Knight and creating a square for the Rook on b1 if Black decides to jump on c2.</skip>

**11...Bb7 12.c5**

Closing the Bishop and attacking the Knight.</skip>

**12...a5 13.Rd1**

Activating the Rook, and targeting the weak d7 pawn.</skip>

**13...Nd5 14.Bd2**

Developing the Bishop, and preparing to recapture on c3 with the Bishop to attack the a5 pawn.</skip>

**14...Ba6 15.Nxd5**

We are spoling Black's pawn structure now, because we are in time with Rac1 to defend c5.</skip>

**15...cxd5 16.Re1**

Overprotecting e5 and Black was threatening Be2.</skip>

**16...Qc7 17.Rac1**

White is better because Black's Pair of Bishops is not active and White has a development advantage plus a better pawn structure.</skip>

### (247) 7. Rossolimo 3...g6 and 4...bx - Rossolimo 6.Qxd4 #8 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6**

**5.d4**

**5...cxd4**

**6.Qxd4**

**6...f6**

Preparing to go Nh6-Nf7 and Bg7, O-O.</skip>

**7.O-O**

Safety first.</skip>

**7...d6 8.c4**

If we don't do this, Black will have better control in the center.</skip>

**8...c5 9.Qd3**

Is the natural square in this pawn structure.</skip>

**9...Bg7 10.Nc3**

Developing the Knight.</skip>

**10...Nh6 11.Nh4**

Preparing to go f4, and if Black goes Nf7, we can even go f5!</skip>

**11...Nf7**

Looks thematic, but White gets a lot of play with f4-f5.</skip>

**12.f4**

Gaining space on the King-side.</skip>

**12...O-O 13.f5**

Blocking the Bishop, and then we can rotate the play on the other side of the board.</skip>

**13...g5 14.Nf3**

Sidestepping the attack of the pawn.</skip>

**14...Rb8 15.h3**

Making a luft.</skip>

**15...e6 16.b3**

Solidifying the Queen-side position.</skip>

**16...Qd7 17.fxe6**

Preparing to jump on d5 with the Knight.</skip>

**17...Qxe6 18.Nd5**

White has a strong Knight on d5, while Black is struggling to finish development. White will probably follow up with Bb2-Rad1, and if Black does nothing, we can rotate the Knight to f5 via e3.</skip>

### (249) 7. Rossolimo 3...g6 and 4...bx - Rossolimo 6.Qxd4 #10

**11...Rb8**

If we do the same f4, Black can counter us with f5, so we have to do something else.</skip>

**12.b3**

Solidifying the Queen-side.</skip>

**12...O-O 13.Re1**

If Black tries to activate the g7 Bishop with f5, our Rook will become active on e1.</skip>

**13...Nf7 14.f4**

Gaining some space.</skip>

**14...f5**

Not clear if we wanted to go f5, but anyway Black has to do something right?I mean the position is solid, but as a human you can't just sit and wait with the board full of pieces.</skip>

**15.exf5**

Opening the e file.</skip>

**15...gxf5 16.Bb2**

Developing the Bishop and defending the Knight.</skip>

**16...e6 17.Nf3**

Improving Knight's position.</skip>

**17...Re8 18.Re3**

We have two backward pawns on d6 and e6 that we can attack, so Black will have to defend those pawns for a long time. ½-½ (64) Kovalev,V (2687) -Praggnanandhaa,R (2539) Wijk aan Zee 2019</skip>

Taking the pawn, forcing Black's Queen to go after it, and then we can attack it.</skip>

Preparing c4 with the idea to castle.</skip>

### (260) 7. Rossolimo 3...g6 and 4...bx - Rossolimo 5...Ba6 #11 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6**

It's a riskier recapture strategically speaking because it creates an isolated pawn on a7 , but on the other hand, it gives Black some dynamic assets, such as having a central pawn on the d-file, that will advance sooner or later. Black usually has two main plans: • To play it slow with: Bg7-d6-Nh6-f6-Nf7-O-O and say that the Bishop Pair is a strong asset when the position is going to open up • To play for a quick 5.-- ...d5 , trying to not give White an easy game</skip>

**5.d4**

**5...Ba6**

**6.dxc5**

**6...Qa5+ 7.Nbd2**

**7...Nf6 8.a3**

**8...Qxc5 9.b4**

**9...Qb6 10.c4**

**10...d5**

**11.e5**

**11...Ne4 12.O-O**

**12...Nxd2 13.Bxd2**

**13...Bxc4**

**14.e6**

**14...Bxf1 15.exf7+**

Opening up the Position.</skip>

**15...Kxf7 16.Ne5+**

Bringing the Knight in the attack.</skip>

**16...Ke8 17.Qf3**

Ignoring the Bishop that is hanging on f1!</skip>

**17...Bb5 18.Qf7+**

Hunting the King.</skip>

**18...Kd8 19.Qe6**

Black is either losing a lot of material, or gets mated.</skip>

**19...Kc7 20.Qd7+**

Forcing the King to go on b8.</skip>

**20...Kb8 21.Bf4**

With mate to follow soon after the Knight discovery check.</skip>

### (319) 8. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 7.Nc3 O-O #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

A move that has been popularized by the legendary Moldovian coach Viacheslav Chebanenko among his students: Viktor Bologan, Sergey Rublevsky, Viorel Iordachescu and others. At first, it looks insane to give up the Bishop in such a way without being provoked by Black, but White is playing for a better pawn structure. Of course, over the years, chess has moved on from such dogma that a Bishop is always better than a Knight, 'whatever the weather'. Giving up the Bishop Pair early on in the game, but on the other hand, we are spoiling Black's pawn structure. Now Black has two recaptures available, both of them leading to highly theoretical debates nowadays. The move 4...dxc6 is by far the most popular recapture, leading to a maneuvering game, with a stable pawn structure, while the 4...bxc6 recapture is riskier and can lead to some very original positions. Recapturing from the side is the favorite way to play for agressive players, or in must-win situations with Black because the play can get very sharp, unlike 4...dxc6. The general issue for Black in capturing from the side though is that the light-squared Bishop is struggling to find a good place.</skip>

**4...dxc6**

The main move, leading to a positional struggle. Let's talk about the position for a bit. So, you can see that Black has the doubled c-pawns, but as compensation Black has the Bishop Pair. If you saw my recommendation against 2...g6, you know that the Bishop Pair is great, and it is, but this position is a little more complex than that. First of all, the position is semi-closed, so that makes it tough for the Bishops; second of all, Black is usually going e5 to step in the center, after which the dark-squared Bishop becomes a little passive. It is not a tragedy for Black because White's main plan is to break with f4, which Black always replies by playing exf4. We know by now that White's main plan is to play for f4 but, if Black goes for the quick 6...b6 line, the plan with a3-b4 becomes viable. Generally, I don't like the a3-b4 plan based on the Wang Hao-Carlsen game because Black gets a lot of dynamic play, but against 6...b6 you can go for it.</skip>

**5.d3 Bg7 6.h3 Nf6 7.Nc3 O-O**

It was considered to be pretty solid, but i think i found a nice and simple plan to get an edge.</skip>

**8.Be3**

Developing the Bishop, attacking the c5 pawn.</skip>

**8...b6 9.a4**

Threatening to play a5 to undermine the c5 pawn, and activate our Rook.</skip>

**9...e5**

Is ignoring White's idea.</skip>

**10.a5**

Threatening to take on b6.</skip>

**10...Rb8 11.axb6**

Activating the Rook.</skip>

**11...axb6 12.O-O**

White has a typical Rossolimo edge, and can follow up with Qd2-Bh6, Ne2, Nh2, and the thematic f4 lever.</skip>

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Bg7 6.h3 Nf6 7.Nc3 O-O**

**8.Be3**

**8...b6 9.a4**

**9...e5**

**10.a5**

**10...Ba6**

Was played in a few correspondence games.</skip>

**11.Qd2**

Developing the Queen, keeping Bh6 ideas.</skip>

**11...Qe7 12.axb6**

Opening the a file.</skip>

**12...axb6 13.O-O**

Getting our King to safety.</skip>

**13...Bb7 14.Bh6**

We are going to trade the Bishops, and follow up with Ne2, Nh2 and push f4.</skip>

Taking the Rook.</skip>

Heading to c4.</skip>

**23.h4**

We can play on both sides of the board!This is the reason why Black's position is difficult.If you only attack on one side, your opponent will usually be able to defend, but if you combine threats on both sides of the board, it's usually much harder to answer.</skip>

**23...Bf6 24.g3**

### (327) 8. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 7.Nc3 O-O #9

### (330) 8. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 7.Nc3 O-O #12 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Bg7 6.h3 Nf6 7.Nc3 O-O**

**8.Be3**

**8...b6 9.a4**

**9...a5**

**10.O-O**

**10...Ne8**

Is the most common plan to go for e5, and then slowly maneuver the Knight via c7-e6-d4.</skip>

**11.Nd2**

My fresh idea! In most of the games, White was playing Qd2, but I think Black has decent counterplay in that line. The point of 11.Nd2!? is to go for f4 next.</skip>

**11...Nc7**

It's crazy how many times i faced this in my blitz games!</skip>

**12.f4**

Taking space in the center.</skip>

**12...f5**

A lot of players find it natural to stop White's plan by going f5, at least in my Blitz Games.The problem for Black though, is that the g7 Bishop will be passive after White goes e5.</skip>

**13.e5**

Taking space, limiting the enemy Bishop.</skip>

**13...Ne6 14.Nf3 Nd4 15.Kh2**

Preparing to play Rg1 and g4 at some point.</skip>

**15...Ra7 16.b3**

Solidifying the Queen-side.</skip>

**16...h6 17.Nb1**

Tough to find this in a blitz game, but easy for Lc0!The Knight will be heading to c4.</skip>

**17...Kh7 18.Nbd2**

Preparing to recapture on f3 with the Knight!</skip>

**18...e6**

Looks to be a normal move, but it actually traps the Knight on d4!</skip>

**19.Ne1**

Black has now to give up a pawn to save the piece, but White is much better.</skip>

### (331) 8. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 7.Nc3 O-O #13 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Bg7 6.h3 Nf6 7.Nc3 O-O**

**8.Be3**

**8...b6 9.a4**

**9...a5**

**10.O-O**

**10...Ne8**

**11.Nd2**

**11...Nc7**

**12.f4**

**12...f5**

**13.e5**

**13...Ne6 14.Nf3 Nd4 15.Kh2**

**15...Ra7 16.b3**

**16...h6 17.Nb1**

**17...Kh7 18.Nbd2**

**18...Rg8**

Black is also preparing g5 at some point if you are not careful.</skip>

**19.Rg1**

Preparing g4 at some point.</skip>

**19...Rd7 20.Rc1**

It's crazy how Lc0 is making small moves to improve the position at maximum and then finally break with g4.</skip>

**20...Bb7 21.Qf1**

The Queen will be best placed on f2.</skip>

**21...e6 22.Nc4**

The Knight is best placed on c4.</skip>

**22...Ba6 23.Qf2**

The best square for the Queen.</skip>

**23...Bf8 24.Rce1**

Now the Rook is making room for the Bishop to go on b2 via-c1.</skip>

**24...Bc8 25.Bc1**

Heading to b2.</skip>

**25...Bb7 26.Bb2**

Now finally White improved all of his pieces at maximum and is ready to play g4 under the best circumstances.</skip>

**26...Ba8 27.g4**

White is clearly better having the initiative on the King-side, plus can consider throwing in Nd6 at any moment; after that, White can look forward to break in the center with d4 (obviously after the Knights will get swapped). So far you have seen a typical Rossolimo squeeze, White was slowly maneuvering and improving his position, while Black with less space could only wait.</skip>

### (335) 8. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 7.Nc3 Nd7 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Bg7 6.h3 Nf6 7.Nc3 Nd7 8.Be3 O-O**

Black is going for a slower route for the Knight.The idea is to go for Re8-Nf8-Ne6-Nd4.This plan is slower than the other route of going via e8-c7-e6, and is allowing us to get a quick f4 with a typical initiative for the Rossolimo.</skip>

**9.Qd2**

Developing the Queen, keeping both options available for castling.</skip>

**9...Re8**

Trying to answer Bh6 with Bh8 but is too slow.</skip>

**10.Bh6**

Threatening to trade the Bishops.</skip>

**10...Bh8 11.h4**

This works well now becaue the enemy Knigh is on d7.</skip>

**11...Nf6**

Would be naive to go after the pawn.</skip>

**12.h5**

Threatening to take on g6 and open up the h file.</skip>

**12...Nxh5 13.Rxh5**

Taking the Knight.</skip>

**13...gxh5 14.Qg5+**

Is a typical checkmate.</skip>

### (346) 8. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 11.Nh2 Nf8 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Bg7 6.h3 Nf6 7.Nc3 Nd7 8.Be3 e5 9.Qd2**

**9...h6**

Stopping White from playing Bh6.</skip>

**10.O-O Qe7**

Used to be the Main-Move for a while, but White gets easy play.</skip>

**11.Nh2**

Preparing to push f4.</skip>

**11...Nf8 12.f4**

The point of going Nh2!</skip>

**12...exf4 13.Rxf4**

Is the old way to recapture.White's point is that will play Rf2 when Black goes g5, and then bring the other with Raf1, saying that White won a tempo.In fact, it's actually true that White wins a tempo, but the problem is that White is not flexible and Black can play Be6 and cancel the pressure on the f file.</skip>

**13...Be6**

Is giving Black an easy game.</skip>

**14.Raf1**

I agree, White's Rooks are looking really nice optically, but in fact they are not doing that much after Black plays Be6.I think it's fair to say that by going Be6, Black won back the tempo lost when White ''outsmarted'' him by taking with the Rook on f4.So i think this should make it clear for everyone that Rxf4 is not a bad move, but it's not flexible as we like to be in the Rossolimo.</skip>

**14...O-O-O 15.R4f2 g5**

The Knight is coming via g6. Take a look at the following game to see why I never recapture on f4 with the Rook. 0-1 (75) Nevednichy,V (2590)-Saric,I (2628) Kozloduy 2013</skip>

### (347) 8. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 10.O-O Qe7 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Bg7 6.h3 Nf6 7.Nc3 Nd7 8.Be3 e5 9.Qd2**

**9...h6**

**10.O-O Qe7**

**11.Nh2**

**11...g5**

Black can do that if he really wants to, but the f5 square will suffer in the long run.</skip>

**12.Ne2**

Going for the f5 square right away via g3.</skip>

**12...Nf8 13.Ng3**

Controlling both weak squares on the King-side.</skip>

**13...Ng6 14.a3**

Now that Black stopped us from going f4 and he will never be able to do any f5 counterplay, we can go for this Queen-side initiative!</skip>

**14...Be6 15.b4**

Now Bc5 becomes a huge threat.</skip>

**15...cxb4 16.axb4**

Recapturing the pawn, threatening Bc5.</skip>

**16...a6 17.Bc5**

Attacking the Queen, stopping Black from castling.</skip>

**17...Qc7 18.Nh5**

### (367) 8. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - 37.Rossolimo 13.Bxf4 Ne6 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Bg7 6.h3 Nf6 7.Nc3 Nd7 8.Be3 e5 9.Qd2**

**9...h6**

**10.O-O b6**

The modern way of handling this line, implemented by Magnus Carlsen. Black is defending the b6 pawn so he can maneuver the Knight to f8-e6-d4. Here I recommend going for the Nh2-f4, since the a3-b4 plan has been refuted by Carlsen in his game against Wang Hao. Black simply ignored White's play on the Queen-side and went Nf8-Ne6 and sacrificed a pawn on the Queen-side to gain the initiative with f5.</skip>

**11.a3**

This plan dropped out of fashion because Black found a very strong reaction:</skip>

**11...Nf8 12.b4 Ne6**

Sacrificing the pawn.</skip>

**13.bxc5 f5**

Black has a lot of play on the King-side. 0-1 (36) Wang,H (2730) -Carlsen,M (2835) St Petersburg 2018 CBM 187 Extra @@StartSquare@@Heine Nielsen,Peter@@EndSquare@@</skip>

### (371) 8. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 13.Bxf4 g5 #1

**14.Bg3**

Preparing to play e5 next!</skip>

**14...Ng6**

### (405) 9. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 5.d3 Qc7 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

Going for the Rossolimo!I've decided to pick this opening because it's giving us the following advantages: -we are avoiding a lot of complex theory -we are going to have a strategical battle (the most sicilian players are very gifted tactical players) -we are going to be better prepared than our opponent ( most of the players are focusing on the Open Sicilians, not on Anti-Sicilians) -we are still playing for an opening advantage, the Rossolimo is the main weapon of top players such as:Fabiano Caruana, Magnus Carlsen, Levon Aronian, Anand Viswanathan, Michael Adams, and so on.</skip>

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Qc7**

An interesting sideline, Black wants to play similar to the English Opening: e5-f6 and then Nh6-Nf7 is a common idea.</skip>

**6.O-O**

We are not afraid of Bg4.</skip>

**6...h6 7.a4**

Grabbing space on the Queen-side.</skip>

**7...g5**

This idea was used by Parham Maghsodloo, but i doubt it's very strong here.</skip>

**8.Nfd2**

Rerouting the Knight to c4 and preparing f4.</skip>

**8...Bg7 9.Nc4**

Preparing f4.</skip>

**9...Be6 10.Nba3**

White is going to push f4 and has a safer King.</skip>

### (406) 9. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 5.d3 Qc7 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Qc7**

**6.O-O**

**6...e5 7.a4**

**7...a5**

Is the common reaction when White goes a4, but this invites White to bring the Knight to c4.</skip>

**8.Na3**

Developing the Knight.</skip>

**8...f6 9.Nd2**

We want to get the f3 Knight to c4, because we are trying to play for f4 as quickly as possible.</skip>

**9...h5 10.Ndc4**

White is ready to play f4, and if he gets it, things are looking pretty ugly for Black with the King still in the center of the board.</skip>

**10...g5**

Has to be played in order to stop White from playing f4.</skip>

**11.Bd2**

Targeting the weak pawn from a5.</skip>

**11...Ne7 12.Qe1**

Threatening to take on a5, and preparing a surprising idea.</skip>

**12...b6 13.h4**

A temporary pawn sacrifice that will help us deliver f4!</skip>

**13...Ng6**

Black tries to sac the pawn.</skip>

**14.hxg5**

Taking the pawn.</skip>

**14...fxg5 15.Bxg5**

Accepting the gift, and preparing f4.</skip>

**15...h4 16.f4**

Opening up the position using a simple tactic.</skip>

**16...Nxf4 17.Nxe5**

A simple tactic to win the pawn.</skip>

**17...Qxe5 18.Bxf4**

White is up a pawn with a safer King.</skip>

**6...e5 7.a4**

**7...f6**

**8.Nfd2**

**8...Nh6**

The typical maneuver, the Knight is heading to f7.</skip>

**9.Nc4**

Improving the Knight and preparing to play f4.</skip>

**9...Nf7 10.a5**

Gaining extra space if Black allows it.</skip>

**10...Be6 11.b3**

Supporting the Knight, preparing to recapture with the b pawn on c4.</skip>

**11...Be7 12.f4**

The typical breakthrough is on the board.</skip>

**12...Bxc4 13.bxc4 exf4 14.Bxf4 Bd6 15.Bxd6 Qxd6 16.Nd2**

The Knight goes to f3.</skip>

**16...Ne5 17.Nf3**

Challenging the powerful e5 Knight!</skip>

**17...O-O 18.Nxe5 Qxe5 19.Rb1**

White's position is more pleasant because the b7 pawn is a target, and the f6 pawn is vulnerable.With no weaknesses in his camp, White is playing for 2 results here.</skip>

White is simply going to play Qg3 and double the Rook on the f file with Raf1 next, while Black's position is simply collapsing.You can see in this exaple how powerful strategical chess is when White is completly winning on move 15 without doing anything more than developing.</skip>

### (420) 9. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 5.d3 Bg4 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

Going for the Rossolimo!I've decided to pick this opening because it's giving us the following advantages: -we are avoiding a lot of complex theory -we are going to have a strategical battle (the most sicilian players are very gifted tactical players) -we are going to be better prepared than our opponent (most of the players are focusing on the Open Sicilians, not on Anti-Sicilians) -we are still playing for an opening advantage, the Rossolimo is the main weapon of top players such as:Fabiano Caruana, Magnus Carlsen, Levon Aronian, Anand Viswanathan, Michael Adams, and so on.</skip>

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Bg4**

Is a little bit too early.</skip>

**6.Nbd2**

White wants to recapture on f3 with the Knight!</skip>

**6...Bg7 7.h3**

Attacking the Bishop.</skip>

**7...Bxf3 8.Nxf3**

Keeping a harmonious set-up!</skip>

**8...Nf6 9.O-O**

**9...Nd7 10.Be3**

Developing the Bishop, keeping an eye on the c5 pawn.</skip>

**10...O-O 11.c3**

Threatening to go d4 and create a nice pawn center.</skip>

**11...e5 12.Qb3**

Attacking the b7 pawn.</skip>

**12...b6 13.a4**

White has the initiative on the Queen-side.</skip>

### (421) 9. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 5.d3 Bg4 #2

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Bg4**

**6.Nbd2**

**6...Bg7 7.h3**

**7...Bxf3 8.Nxf3**

**8...Nf6 9.O-O**

**9...Nd7 10.Be3**

**10...O-O 11.c3**

**11...e5 12.Qb3**

**12...Qb6 13.Qc2**

White has the simple plan of going a4-a5, Nd2-c4, and the thematic pawn lever with f4.</skip>

### (422) 9. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 6.h3 b6 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Bg7 6.h3 b6**

Black is trying to predict that we are going to play Be3, and have the c5 pawn protected.Once we go for Be3, Black's idea is to play e5 and put the Knight on e7, following up with a quick f5.This plan can be quite dangerous if it catches you off-guard. What you need to know is that this plan fails to the classical expansion on the Queen-side with a3-b4.Note that this is the only line in this repertoire where i recommend the a3-b4 plan!</skip>

**7.O-O**

Getting our King to safety.</skip>

**7...Nh6**

Is played sometimes with the idea of going f6-Nf7-e5 and stay solid.</skip>

**8.a4**

Gaining space on the Queen-side!</skip>

**8...f6 9.a5**

White is dominating the whole board.</skip>

**9...Rb8 10.e5**

A strong move, stopping Black from going e5 himself!</skip>

**10...Nf7 11.exf6**

Taking the pawn.</skip>

**11...exf6 12.Re1+**

White is much better because Black has no development.</skip>

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Bg7 6.h3 Nh6**

**7.a4**

**7...f6**

**8.a5**

**8...Rb8**

Forced so Black can answer Be3 with b6.</skip>

**9.O-O**

Getting our King to safety.</skip>

**9...Nf7 10.Be3**

Developing the Bishop, attacking the c5 pawn.</skip>

**10...b6 11.axb6**

Taking the pawn, activating our Rook.</skip>

**11...axb6 12.Ra7**

Invading on the seventh rank.</skip>

**12...O-O 13.Nbd2**

Developing the Knight, heading to c4.</skip>

**13...e5 14.Nc4**

Activating the Knight.</skip>

**14...Be6 15.b3**

We always want to recapture on c4 with the b pawn, if possible!</skip>

**15...Re8 16.Qa1**

Preparing to triple!</skip>

**16...Re7 17.Qa3**

Preparing to bring the f1 Rook in the fight for the a file!</skip>

**17...Reb7 18.Ra1 Qc7**

Black can only wait and try to defend.</skip>

**19.Rxb7**

Trading a pair of Rooks, so we can invade with the Queen.</skip>

**19...Qxb7 20.Qa7**

White has a typical Rossolimo edge with a small but steady pressure along the a file.The simple threat of trading the Queens and playing Ra6 to attack the b6 pawn is very effective.</skip>

### (443) 9. Rossolimo,3...g6 and 4...dxc6 - Rossolimo 6.h3 e5 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...g6 4.Bxc6**

**4...dxc6**

**5.d3 Bg7 6.h3 e5**

This line is similar with 6...b6 because they can transpose to each other, but this one is trickyer since Black has some extra options, although the main point remains the same: -if White is naive and rushes with Be3, Black can get quick counterplay based on f5.So as a general rule for White to stay out of trouble, is to gain space on the Queen-side first with a4, and then bring the b1 Knight to c4.This would cause Black enough headaches to slow him down a bit with the King-Side initiative.</skip>

**7.a4**

Gaining space on the Queen-side, more or less forcing Black to play a5.</skip>

**7...Qe7**

Is a little to passive.</skip>

**8.a5**

Taking even more space if Black allows us to.</skip>

**8...Nf6 9.Be3 Nd7 10.Nbd2 Nf8**

Black is maneuvering the Knight to e6.</skip>

**11.Nc4 f6**

Defending the pawn so Black can go Ne6.</skip>

**12.h4**

Destabilizing Black's position!</skip>

**12...Ne6 13.h5 g5 14.Nfd2**

White is going to maneuver the Knight to f5 with a better position.</skip>

### (469) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 4.Bxc6 dxc6 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

A very dynamical line! We can draw this conclusion by looking at the main ambassadors of this line: the Magician from Riga Alexei Shirov, and El Niño from Baku Teimour Radjabov, both known for their hyper-aggressive playing style. I think this line reached its peak in 2012 when Boris Gelfand used it 3 times in his World Championship match against Vishy Anand. From that point on the line has not been repeated in a World Championship Match, but it's still played quite often at all levels. Black prepares to bring his King's Knight to e7 and then to put the question to White's Bishop, with the move ...a7-a6. In this case, the exchange on c6 will be favourable for Black, because he will recapture with the Knight and keep a healthy pawn structure.</skip>

**4.Bxc6**

This is the move which Viacheslav Andreevich Chebanenko used to recommend to his pupils. Nothing is attacking the Bishop on b5, but nevertheless, White voluntarily exchanges it for the Knight. Now a very interesting position arises, with Black having doubled pawns. In addition, in this structure, he will not find it easy to activate his Bishop on c8.</skip>

**4...dxc6**

It's a worse version compared to 3...g6 and 4...dxc6 because the Bishop on c8 is locked.</skip>

**5.O-O Nf6 6.Re1 Be7 7.d3 h6 8.e5 Nd5 9.Nbd2 a5 10.Ne4**

White played Nfd2 and Qh5 with play on the King-side in the game: 1-0 (34) Fedoseev,V (2674) -Korobkov,P (2423) Jurmala 2015</skip>

### (470) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 4.Bxc6 dxc6 #2 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...dxc6**

**5.O-O Ne7 6.d3 Ng6 7.e5**

again, this thematic push will help us to restrict Black's pieces and we have created a nice outpost for our Knight via c3-d4.</skip>

**7...Be7 8.Nc3 O-O 9.Re1 b6 10.Qe2 Bd7 11.a4 a5 12.g3**

We restrict their g6 Knight and we can think about Nd2-c4.</skip>

**12...f6 13.exf6 Bxf6 14.Ne4**

Black has a weak isolated pawn on e6.1-0 (47) Horwitz,D (2291)-Dewald,H (1869) ICCF email 2017</skip>

### (471) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 4.Bxc6 dxc6 #3 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...dxc6**

**5.O-O Ne7 6.d3 Ng6 7.e5**

**7...Be7 8.Nc3 O-O 9.Re1 f6 10.exf6 gxf6 11.h4**

The star move, when the Knight is on g6.</skip>

**11...Rf7 12.h5 Nf8 13.Bh6 e5 14.Nh4**

White took advantage of the light squares in the game: 1-0 (63) Naiditsch,A (2665)-Kovac,B (2336) Mainz 2008</skip>

### (472) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 4.Bxc6 bxc6#1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.b3 f6**

But this move that is limiting our Bishop is also quite annoying.</skip>

### (473) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 4.Bxc6 bxc6 #2 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.b3 e5**

Was Gelfand's weapon.</skip>

### (474) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

I decided to pick this line over 5.b3 that was played in the match for the World Crown in 2012 between Anand and Gelfand. The point of 5.d3 is that we are solidifying the e4 pawn, and we are staying flexible with the Bishop. You will see that in some positions it makes sense to go for b3-Bb2.</skip>

**5...Qc7**

The favorite move of Magnus Carlsen nowadays! The point is to meet the flexible Qe2 with e5.</skip>

**6.Qe2 e5**

This move becomes very decent now with the Queen on e2, because if we go for the same plan of pushing c3-d4, the Queen will not be ideally placed on e2, after opening up the center.</skip>

### (475) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #2 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6.O-O**

Avoiding the trap for now.</skip>

**6...e5**

It's not great with the Queen on the initial square because White can quickly get in c3-d4!</skip>

**7.c3 Nf6 8.d4 cxd4 9.cxd4 d6 10.dxe5**

A nice positional decision, creating the isolated pawn on c6.</skip>

**10...dxe5 11.Qc2 Bd6 12.Bg5 Nd7 13.Nbd2 f6 14.Be3 O-O 15.Rac1**

White has free play against the isolated pawn.</skip>

### (476) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #3 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6.O-O**

**6...d6**

The plan of going d6-e5 is inferior here, because White has the simple and effective plan of going c3-d4.</skip>

**7.c3 Nf6 8.Re1 Rb8 9.h3**

Preventing Black from playing any Bg4 in the future.</skip>

**9...e5 10.d4 cxd4 11.cxd4 Be7 12.dxe5**

Creating an isolated pawn on c6, that we are going to play against.</skip>

**12...dxe5 13.Qc2 O-O 14.b3 Re8 15.Nbd2 Nh5 16.Nc4 f6 17.Be3 Bf8 18.Rac1**

White was pressing the weakness from c6 in the game: ½-½ (36) Nevednichy,V (2601)-Conquest,S (2563) Novi Sad 2009</skip>

### (477) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #4 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6.O-O**

**6...d6**

**7.c3 Nf6 8.Re1 e5 9.d4 cxd4 10.cxd4 Bg4 11.Nbd2**

Keeping a nice coordination, threatening to recapture on f3 with the Knight and having good control in the center.</skip>

**11...Be7 12.h3 Bh5 13.Qc2 O-O 14.b3**

We prepare to bring our Bishop to another diagonal and connect the Rooks.</skip>

**14...Rac8 15.Bb2 Nd7 16.Rac1**

White has a strong center and pressure on the c6 pawn. White went on to win in the game: 1-0 (43) Grischuk,A (2720)-Zhang,P (2613) Beersheba 2005</skip>

### (478) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #5 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6.O-O**

**6...Be7 7.e5**

Locking the light squared Bishop and making it tough for Black to develop.</skip>

**7...f6 8.Bf4 Qd8 9.Nfd2 fxe5 10.Qh5+**

An important check, forcing Black to make a concession.</skip>

**10...g6 11.Qxe5 Nf6 12.Ne4 O-O 13.Bh6 d6 14.Qc3 Re8 15.Nbd2**

White is better with a safer King. Black managed to save the game in correspondence chess, but I think it's not that simple for a human to play with Black. ½-½ (61) Lanc,A (2494)-Oskulski,J (2495) ICCF email 2007</skip>

### (479) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #6 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6.O-O**

**6...Ne7 7.h4**

This crazy idea was implemented by the biggest Rossolimo expert at the moment, Fabiano Caruana against no other than Magnus Carlsen! Anand also used it a month later to defeat the biggest expert of this line from the Black side, Boris Gelfand. This h4 move is generally very natural in this structure, trying to harass the Knight that is trying to find a safe place on g6. The crazy part is that White can afford to do this even after castling short!</skip>

**7...h5**

Has never been played, but i think it is the standard reaction to h4.</skip>

**8.e5**

Locking the Bishop on c8.</skip>

**8...f6 9.Re1 Ng6 10.Na3**

Going to c4 via a3, to not block the c1 Bishop. This move is a pawn sacrifice, I think we get good compensation because Black's King will struggle to find a safe place.</skip>

**10...fxe5 11.Nc4 d6 12.Bg5**

The most active square for the Bishop, waiting for Black to play Be7 and trade the dark squared Bishop, so we can plant the Knight on g5!</skip>

**12...Ba6 13.Qe2 Bxc4 14.dxc4**

Usually you want to recapture toward the center, but we don't mind dxc4 here because we can use the d file to attack the d6 pawn.</skip>

**14...Rb8 15.Rad1**

Sacrificing the b2 pawn for activity.</skip>

**15...Rxb2 16.Qd3 Qf7 17.Qe4 Rb6 18.Nxe5**

White takes advantage of the back-ranker and has the initiative because Black is struggling to develop his King-side.</skip>

### (480) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #7 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6.O-O**

**6...Ne7 7.h4**

**7...h5**

**8.e5**

**8...f6 9.Re1 Ng6 10.Na3**

**10...fxe5 11.Nc4 d6 12.Bg5**

**12...Ba6 13.Qe2 Bxc4 14.dxc4**

**14...Qf7 15.Rad1 Qf5 16.b4**

A surprising breakthrough! Black's King is vulnerable in the center of the board.</skip>

**16...cxb4 17.c5**

Opening the Queen's path to the Black King.</skip>

**17...dxc5 18.Qa6**

Black is up 3 pawns at the moment, but the game might not last very long from now on. The fact that the King-side is blocked, is fully compensating for White's sacrifice.</skip>

**13...Be7 14.Bxe7 Qxe7 15.Qf3 Bb7 16.a3**

We are going to play b4 next and undermine Black's pawn structure.</skip>

### (482) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #9

**7...h5**

**8.e5**

**10...d5 11.exd6**

Creating doubled isolated pawns on the c-file.</skip>

**11...Bxd6 12.Nc4 e5 13.Nxd6+ Qxd6 14.Nd2**

Not allowing Black to pin us with Bg4 even at the cost of a pawn!</skip>

**14...Nxh4 15.Ne4 Qd5 16.c4 Qd7 17.f4**

We can take back the pawn on c5 anytime, and the enemy King is still in the center of the board.</skip>

### (483) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #10 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6.O-O**

**7...h5**

**8.e5**

**10...d5 11.exd6**

**11...Bxd6 12.Nc4 Be7 13.Qe2 e5 14.Bd2 Bg4 15.Qe4**

An important move attacking the Knight, and breaking the pin.</skip>

**15...Kf7 16.Bc3 Rhe8 17.g3**

Defending the pawn, preparing to maneuver the Knight.</skip>

**17...Be6 18.Nfd2 Qd7 19.Kh2 Bd5 20.Qe2 Rh8 21.Qf1**

We are going to play f3, double on the e-file, and slowly build up the pressure.</skip>

### (484) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #11 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6.O-O**

**6...Ne7 7.h4**

**7...h5**

**8.e5**

**8...f6 9.Re1 Ng6 10.Na3**

**10...Be7 11.Nc4 Ba6 12.b3**

Inviting Black to take the Knight because we can use the b file later!</skip>

**12...Bxc4 13.bxc4 Nxe5 14.Nxe5 fxe5 15.g3**

Defending the h4 pawn.</skip>

**15...d6 16.Qf3 Rb8 17.Bd2**

We are going to play Rab1-Rb3-Reb1, because doubling on the b-file will put a lot of pressure on Black's position. Imagine we just teleport the Rooks to b1 and b3, then Ba5!? could be a threat because the enemy King is weak, and all kinds of tactics will appear because Black is struggling to coordinate his pieces.</skip>

### (485) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #12 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6.O-O**

**6...Ne7 7.h4**

**7...h5**

**8.e5**

**8...f6 9.Re1 Ng6 10.Na3**

**10...Be7 11.Nc4 Ba6 12.b3**

**12...Bxc4 13.bxc4 fxe5 14.g3**

Defending the pawn.</skip>

**14...d6 15.Bg5 Bf6 16.Rb1**

Black is a pawn up, but he will never castle and if we don't somehow trade all the pieces, the pawn deficit is not felt. We could consider to break with c3-d4 here since the e6 pawn is weak, or simply play Rb3 and try to double on the b-file later.</skip>

### (486) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #13 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6.O-O**

**6...Ne7 7.h4**

**7...e5 8.h5 h6**

Black is threatening to meet Nh2 with g5!</skip>

**9.Nh4**

This move is important because Black's source of counterplay is to go g5 after we play f4 exf4 Bxf4. So with the Knight on h4, we can trade the Knights on g6 after hxg6!</skip>

**9...d6 10.f4 exf4 11.Bxf4 g5 12.hxg6 Nxg6 13.Nxg6**

That is the point that i was talking about after 9.Nh4!</skip>

**13...fxg6 14.Nd2**

Black's King is in trouble.</skip>

### (487) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Do not play Nh2 here. 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6.O-O**

**6...Ne7 7.h4**

**7...e5 8.h5 h6**

**9.Nh2 d6 10.f4 exf4 11.Bxf4 g5**

Was already shown by Gelfand to give Black enough counterplay: 1-0 (49) Anand,V (2753)-Gelfand,B (2676) chess24.com INT 2020</skip>

### (488) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #14 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6.O-O**

**6...Ne7 7.h4**

**7...e5 8.h5 d6 9.Nh2**

Now that the c8 Bishop is opened Black can use the g4 square after he reroutes the Knight to f6, so we need to control that square by going Nh2!</skip>

**9...Ng8 10.f4 c4 11.dxc4 Nf6 12.Nc3**

Notice that here White is simply up a pawn and Black has no threats.</skip>

### (489) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #15 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6.O-O**

**6...Ne7 7.h4**

**7...e5 8.h5 Ng8 9.Nh2**

Black is going to play Nf6 next and could follow it up with d6, transposing to 8...d6, that is why we need to take control over the g4 square here as well by playing Nh2! The same logic applies here as in the 8...d6 line. These lines could very well transpose into each other.</skip>

**9...Nf6 10.f4 c4**

Does not really work that well.</skip>

**11.h6**

Destabilizing Black's King-side!</skip>

**11...cxd3 12.cxd3 Rg8 13.Nc3 gxh6 14.fxe5 Qxe5 15.Bf4**

Developing the Bishop with tempo.</skip>

**15...Bc5+ 16.Kh1 Qh5 17.Rf3**

We are going to play d4 next whatever Black does, and White will be better with a solid pawn center.</skip>

### (490) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #16

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Qc7**

**6...Ne7 7.h4**

**7...e5 8.h5 Ng8 9.Nh2**

Developing the Queen and waiting to see where is Black willing to castle.</skip>

**12...exf4 13.Bxf4 O-O 14.h6**

Undermining the f6 Knight!</skip>

**14...g6 15.Bg5 Ne8 16.Bxe7 Qxe7 17.Rae1**

We are threatening to play e5 next and the enemy King is weak.</skip>

### (491) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Qc7 #17 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

A nice prophylactic move taking away the g5 square, not allowing Black to attack our important pawn from h6.</skip>

**17...Nf4 18.Ne2**

Trying to get rid of the dangerous Knight.</skip>

**18...Ne6 19.Ng4**

### (493) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...d5 #2

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...d5**

**6.Qe2 Nf6 7.c4**

Fixing the weakness from c5.</skip>

**7...Be7 8.O-O O-O 9.Bg5**

We are going to prove that the Knights are stronger than the Bishops in a closed position!</skip>

**9...h6 10.Bxf6 Bxf6 11.e5**

Closing the Bishop!</skip>

**11...Be7 12.Nc3**

The Knight is heading to a4 from where it's going to attack the weak c5 pawn.</skip>

**12...Rb8 13.Na4 Ba6 14.Rac1 d4**

Would be a dream for us to get the e4 square!</skip>

### (494) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...d5 #3

**5...d5**

**6.Qe2 Nf6 7.c4**

**7...Be7 8.O-O O-O 9.Bg5**

### (495) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Ne7 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Ne7**

The old main line.</skip>

**6.Qe2**

They teach you to develop the Queen last, so what is wrong with me that I am even adding an ''!'' to this move?! Well, here is the thing: the general rule is correct, and it applies in most of the cases, but unlike maths, chess is a sport, not a science. 6.Qe2 is a multi-purpose move, preparing to play e5 in some positions taking control over the dark squares, but most important, is that White stays flexible and is waiting for Black to commit, so for example once Black goes for Ng6, we are immediately pushing the h pawn down the board!</skip>

**6...Qc7**

A flexible move, but it allows us to start playing on the King-side.</skip>

**7.h4 e5 8.h5**

We only want to take away the g6 square of the Knight, we are not really looking to push it forward with h6.</skip>

**8...d6 9.Na3 Bg4**

9...Be6 Would help us with our plan. 10.Ng5 Bd7 11.f4 and we will open the file with a strong attack.</skip>

**10.Nc4 f6 11.Ne3 Be6 12.Nd2**

We are going to push with g3 and f4.</skip>

### (496) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...Ne7 #2 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Ne7**

**6.Qe2**

**6...Qc7**

**7.h4 e5 8.h5**

**8...d6 9.Na3 f6 10.Nh2**

Now this plan is the most effective because Black committed to f6, and there will be no good counterplay with g5.</skip>

**10...Be6 11.f4 exf4 12.Bxf4 g5 13.Bg3**

Black has to deal with both f6 and d6.</skip>

### (497) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...f6 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...f6 6.Nh4**

Threatening a nasty check on h5 and preparing to push the f-pawn.</skip>

**6...g6 7.f4 c4 8.Nc3**

### (498) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...f6 #2 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...f6 6.Nh4**

**6...g6 7.O-O**

Very important to not rush with the f-pawn!</skip>

**7...Bg7 8.f4**

Now we are safe with this move! If Black goes c4 we can simply ignore it.</skip>

**8...f5 9.Nf3 Nh6 10.e5**

With a clear space advantage. One of our Knights will land on c4 dominating the center of the board.</skip>

### (501) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...d6 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...d6 6.e5**

Locking the Bishop on c8 and threatening to ruin Black's pawn structure.</skip>

**6...dxe5 7.Nxe5 Qd5 8.Nf3 c4 9.Nc3 Bb4 10.Bd2 Bxc3 11.Bxc3 Ne7 12.dxc4 Qxc4 13.Qd4**

Forcing a better endgame.</skip>

**13...Qxd4 14.Bxd4 f6 15.O-O-O**

White is more active and has a better pawn structure.</skip>

### (502) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 5...d6 #2

**4.Bxc6**

### (503) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo Mainline 5...Ne7 6.Q 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Ne7**

**6.Qe2**

**6...d6**

Has been played by Gelfand, and Black managed to hold his own in correspondence chess as well.</skip>

**7.O-O e5**

Trying to solve the problem of the c8 Bishop, but it allows us to play in the center.</skip>

**8.c3**

Threatening to open up the center with d4 and take advantage of the small lead in development that White has.</skip>

**8...Ba6 9.Rd1**

We are sidestepping the a6-f1 diagonal, so we can continue our plan of pushing d4.</skip>

**9...g6 10.Qc2 Bg7 11.d4 cxd4 12.cxd4 exd4 13.Nxd4**

Threatening to take the c6 pawn.</skip>

**13...Bb7 14.Bf4 O-O 15.Nb3**

Attacking the d6 pawn.</skip>

**15...Nc8 16.Nc3**

White was better because Black's pieces are tied down to defend the d6 pawn, and went on to win in: 1-0 (24) Liang,A (2592)-Niemann,H (2467) Saint Louis 2019</skip>

### (504) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo Mainline 5...Ne7 6.Q 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Ne7**

**6.Qe2**

**6...d6**

**7.O-O f6 8.Rd1**

I decided to recommend this plan because we are going to push d4, and get an easier game with the upcoming Maroczy/Hedgehog type of structure. I think this pawn structure is similar to the one from the 4...bxc6 line of the 3...g6 Rossolimo. Even though, here the pawns are a little bit more advanced by Black and he is controlling more squares, the other side of the coin being that he is lacking development.</skip>

**8...Ng6 9.d4 cxd4 10.Nxd4 c5 11.Nf3**

I think the Knight stays best here because we will play h4 later, and it also allows us to set-up the Maroczy structure nicely.</skip>

**11...Be7 12.c4 Bb7 13.Nc3 a5 14.b3 Qc8 15.Rb1 O-O 16.h4**

With play against the d6 pawn, and potentially on the King-side.</skip>

White is hunting the g6 Knight with h5 next, and also the d6 pawn is vulnerable. White has a safe game here with no weaknesses. Black managed to hold in a correspondence game, but I think it would be way trickier for a human to defend here. Beveridge, C. (2153) - Fremmegaard, J. (2225) ICCF email, 2018.33 moves, (½-½).</skip>

### (518) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 6.Qe2 Ng6 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Ne7**

**6.Qe2**

**6...Ng6**

We have to pay attention now because Black is threatening to play 7...e5! and then d6, to activate the c8 Bishop!</skip>

**7.h4**

This should be the first thing that pops out to your mind when you see a Knight on g6! Most of the times you will force Black to play h5 and weaken the g5 square, or if he doesn't react, you can kick that Knight with h5, and put him in an awkward situation.</skip>

**7...e5**

Does not work now because of:</skip>

**8.h5 Nf4 9.Bxf4**

Getting rid of the annoying Knight.</skip>

**9...exf4 10.e5**

We are going to collect the pawn with Qe4-Qxf4.</skip>

### (526) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 6.Qe2 Ng6 #9 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**4.Bxc6**

**5...Ne7**

**6.Qe2**

**6...Ng6**

**7.h4**

**7...h5 8.e5**

''Removing peg by peg!'' A Romanian saying meaning that we are combating fire with fire! Black was threatening to play e5, and open up the c8 Bishop, so actually 8.e5! is a prophylactical move directed against the light-squared Bishop.</skip>

**8...d5 9.exd6**

Creating doubled isoalted pawns on the c file.</skip>

**9...Bxd6 10.Na3 e5 11.Ng5**

Not allowing Black to pin us with Bg4.</skip>

**11...Bc7 12.Nc4 Qd5 13.f3 O-O 14.Be3**

We are going to castle long and attack Black on the King-side.</skip>

### (527) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 6.Qe2 Ng6 #10 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Ne7**

**6.Qe2**

**6...Ng6**

**7.h4**

**7...h5 8.e5**

**8...Be7 9.Qe4**

Centralizing the Queen, stopping any f6 ideas to break the center.</skip>

**9...c4**

Black is trying to confuse us, but the sacrifice is not impressive.</skip>

**10.dxc4 Rb8 11.b3 Bb7 12.Nbd2 d5 13.exd6**

This is very thematic in our preparation. We want to split their pawns and our Rook is not in danger, we can always play Rb1 if neccesary.</skip>

**13...Bf6 14.Rb1 c5 15.Qe3 Qxd6 16.Ne4**

Forcing Black to concede the Bishop Pair.</skip>

**16...Bxe4 17.Qxe4 Bc3+ 18.Ke2**

White's King was safe on e2, and with a pawn up went on to win in the game: 1-0 (30) Popov,V (2557)-Alekseenko,K (2447) St Petersburg 2013</skip>

### (529) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 6.Qe2 Ng6 #12 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Ne7**

**6.Qe2**

**6...Ng6**

**7.h4**

**7...h5 8.e5**

**8...f6**

Black is trying to challenge our center, but now you can see better why h4-h5 was a good inclusion for White:</skip>

**9.Qe4**

We know that the Knight is not defended on g6, now Black has to make an awkward move to defend the Knight.</skip>

**9...f5**

Would have been even worse to completely give up the tension in the center.</skip>

**10.Qe2 Be7 11.Na3**

It is better to go via this route to c4, because if you go for the other route (Nd2-Nc4) the c1 Bishop will be closed for one move. In our case, it might not make a huge difference, but in other scenarios, the small details make the difference.</skip>

**11...Nxh4 12.Nxh4 Bxh4 13.Nc4 g6 14.Nd6+ Kf8 15.Be3**

White will take the c5 pawn next. Black is struggling with the passive light-squared Bishop, and the ''thorn'' on d6. White went on to win in: 1-0 (44) Dobrowolski,P (2388) -Rutkowski,M (2358) Lubniewice 2005</skip>

### (530) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 6.Qe2 Ng6 #13 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Ne7**

**6.Qe2**

**6...Ng6**

**7.h4**

**7...h5 8.e5**

**8...f6**

**9.Qe4**

**9...Kf7**

A sad necessity, but the lesser evil.</skip>

**10.Na3**

It is better to go via this route to c4, because if you go for the other route (Nd2-Nc4) the c1 Bishop will be closed for one move. In our case, it might not make a huge difference, but in other scenarios, the small details are making the difference.</skip>

**10...Ba6 11.Rh3**

Another benefit of 8.h4! The Rook comes very quickly now to attack the enemy King. White is threatening Ng5.</skip>

**11...Qb8 12.Ng5+ fxg5 13.Rf3+ Nf4 14.hxg5**

We are picking up the Knight next, with a better position.</skip>

**9...Kf7**

The Key move behind our strategy! Now Black has to deal with doubled isolated pawns on the c file.</skip>

**11...Qxd6 12.Nc4**

Attacking the Queen.</skip>

**12...Qd5 13.Ne3**

Offering a Queen trade in our terms! If Black wants to get rid of the Queens, he has to remain with the ugly pawn structure!</skip>

### (533) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 6.Qe2 Ng6 #16 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Ne7**

**6.Qe2**

**6...Ng6**

**7.h4**

**7...h5 8.e5**

**8...f6**

**9.Qe4**

**9...Kf7**

**10.Na3**

**10...d5 11.exd6**

**11...Qxd6 12.Nc4**

**12...Qd5 13.Ne3**

**13...Qxe4 14.dxe4**

Let's analyze this endgame, because the strategy is important in this type of positions. White is better with a lot of moves, but the overall strategy is important to maximize your chances to convert. At first glance it looks like a symmetrical position, Black has the doubled isolated pawns, but on the other hand he has the Bishop Pair so you may think that the situation is pretty balanced. Now that we dive a little bit deeper in the position, we can notice that the Bishop Pair is not an important factor here since the f8 Bishop is restricted by its own pawns, and also the c8 Bishop is lacking a useful diagonal. Now that we clarified that the Bishop Pair is not an asset in this position, we see that Black is left with the double isolated pawns on the c file. As a long term plan for White, the Bishop will be nicely placed on a3, from where it attacks the c5 pawn. The King will castle Queen-side, and the f3 Knight will be maneuvered to c4 via d2.</skip>

**14...Ba6 15.b3 e5 16.g3**

We are going to maneuver the f3 Knight to c4, develop the Bishop to b2, and castle Queen-side. Meanwhile, Black is struggling to find a plan.</skip>

**11...Qxd6 12.Nc4**

**12...Qd5 13.Ne3**

**13...Qxe4 14.dxe4**

**14...e5**

We will keep this move as the Main Line because it was played by the Magician from Riga, Alexei Shirov.</skip>

**15.g3**

A solid positional move, not allowing any Nf4 jumps.</skip>

**15...Be6 16.Nd2 Ne7 17.b3 Nc8 18.Bb2**

Topalov, V. (2739) - Shirov, A. (2715) Monte Carlo, 2002.39 moves (½ -½ ).</skip>

### (535) 10. Rossolimo,3...e6 4.Bxc6 - Rossolimo 6.Qe2 Ng6 #18 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e6**

**4.Bxc6**

**4...bxc6 5.d3**

**5...Ne7**

**6.Qe2**

**6...Ng6**

**7.h4**

**7...h5 8.e5**

**8...f6**

**9.Qe4**

**9...Kf7**

**10.Na3**

**10...d5 11.exd6**

**11...Qxd6 12.Nc4**

**12...Qd5 13.Ne3**

**13...Qxe4 14.dxe4**

**14...e5**

**15.g3**

**15...Be6 16.Nd2 Ne7 17.b3 Nc8 18.a4**

Another good positional move taking space on the Queen-side before playing Ba3. Going Ba3 without a4 is not a big mistake, but the Bishop in front of the pawn was a little bit dodgy. Now after a4, Ba3 will come in the best version possible. Going a4 has also a dynamic plus because we can kick the enemy Knight with a5 if Black tries to play Nb6 and stop us from going Nc4.</skip>

**18...Nd6 19.Ba3**

Attacking the c5 pawn.</skip>

**19...Nb7 20.Ndc4**

White is slowly going to squeeze Black because there is no constructive plan for him and can only sit and wait for us to break. We are slowly going to improve our position with moves such as Ke2, Rad1, and at some point look to break with f4.</skip>

### (543) 11. Rossolimo 3...d6 - Rossolimo 4.O-O Bg4 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

Going for the Rossolimo! I've decided to pick this opening because it's giving us the following advantages: • We avoid a lot of complex theory • We are going to have a strategical battle (most Sicilian players are very gifted tactical players!) • We are going to be better prepared than our opponent (most of the players are focusing on the Open Sicilians, not on Anti-Sicilians.) • We are still playing for an opening advantage, the Rossolimo is the main weapon of top players such as Fabiano Caruana, Magnus Carlsen, Levon Aronian, Viswanathan Anand, Michael Adams, and many more.</skip>

**3...d6 4.O-O Bg4 5.h3**

Attacking the Bishop.</skip>

**5...Bh5 6.c3**

Preparing d4.</skip>

**6...Qb6 7.Na3**

Defending the Bishop, and preparing d4.</skip>

**7...Nf6 8.d4**

Threatening d5.</skip>

**8...cxd4 9.cxd4**

Recapturing the pawn, and threatening d5.</skip>

**9...d5 10.e5**

Attacking the Knight.</skip>

**10...Nd7 11.g4**

Attacking the Bishop.</skip>

**11...Bg6 12.e6**

A typical positional sacrifice to keep the Bishop passive on f8.</skip>

**12...fxe6 13.Ng5**

White is going to recapture the pawn with a much better position.</skip>

**11...dxe5 12.Nc4**

**12...Qc5 13.Nxe5**

White has a huge lead in development.</skip>

### (551) 11. Rossolimo 3...d6 - Rossolimo 4.O-O Bd7 5.Re1 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...d6 4.O-O Bd7 5.Re1**

Creating a square for the Bishop on f1.</skip>

**5...Nf6 6.h3**

An interesting move order, if White goes 6.c3 a6 7.Bf1 Black usually follows up with the solid 7...Bg4 .</skip>

**6...a6 7.Bf1**

The safest square.</skip>

**7...g6 8.c3**

Preparing the typical Ruy Lopez play with c3-d4.</skip>

**8...Bg7 9.d4**

Grabbing space in the center.</skip>

**9...O-O 10.dxc5**

I like this approach because as 1.e4 players, there is no need to enter Benoni structures, even though it might be an improved Benoni, i think we can keep an open game and still be ambitious.</skip>

**10...dxc5 11.Bf4**

The point! Taking away the c7 square for the Black Queen.</skip>

**11...Be6 12.Qc2**

The Queen is very well placed here, and we can prepare the Queen-side play based on Nbd2-Nc4, a4-a5.</skip>

**12...h6 13.Nbd2**

**13...Nd7 14.Nc4**

Threatening the positional a4!</skip>

**14...b5 15.Ne3**

White is going to play Rad1 next and jump with the Knight on d5 sooner or later.</skip>

### (582) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Sidelines 3...Qb6 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

Going for the Rossolimo! I've decided to pick this opening because it's giving us the following advantages: • We avoid a lot of complex theory • We are going to have a strategical battle (most Sicilian players are very gifted tactical players!) • We are going to be better prepared than our opponent (most of the players are focusing on the Open Sicilians, not on Anti-Sicilians.) • We are still playing for an opening advantage, the Rossolimo is the main weapon of top players such as Fabiano Caruana, Magnus Carlsen, Levon Aronian, Anand Viswanathan, Michael Adams, and so on.</skip>

**3...Qb6**

Is pretty similar to 3...Qc7, but the difference is that this move is attacking the Bishop on b5.</skip>

**4.Nc3**

Developing the Knight, defending the Bishop, and threating Nd5 sometimes.</skip>

**4...Nd4 5.Nxd4**

Creating doubled pawns for Black on the d file.</skip>

**5...cxd4 6.Nd5**

Attacking the Queen, threatening to fork Black on c7 if he takes the Bishop.</skip>

**6...Qxb5 7.Nc7+ Kd8 8.Nxb5**

White is up a Queen.</skip>

### (583) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Sidelines 3...Qb6 #2 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...Qb6**

**4.Nc3**

**4...Nd4 5.Nxd4**

**5...cxd4 6.Nd5**

**6...Qd8 7.Qh5**

Threatening to play Qe5, and fork Black on c7.</skip>

**7...e6 8.Qe5**

The fork on c7 is unstoppable.</skip>

### (584) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Sidelines 3...Qb6 #3

### (591) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Sidelines 3...Nd4 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...Nd4**

Should not be underestimated! This move is obviously inferior to the mainlines, but still it requires a good reaction by White for an edge. This line reminds me of the Bird Variation of the Ruy Lopez, but I think it's an inferior version here since Black still has to make another move, in order to develop the f8 Bishop.</skip>

**4.Nxd4**

Creating doubled pawns for Black.</skip>

**4...cxd4 5.c3**

This is accurate! Forcing Black to show his hand.</skip>

**5...a6 6.Ba4**

The natural retreat, preparing O-O or Bc2 if Black goes b5.</skip>

**6...b5 7.Bc2**

Now the e4 pawn is protected in case Black goes Bb7.</skip>

**7...dxc3 8.Nxc3**

Recapturing the pawn, developing the Knight.</skip>

**8...Bb7 9.O-O**

**9...e6 10.d4**

Creating a strong pawn center.</skip>

**10...Rc8 11.Be3**

**11...Bb4 12.a4**

We are not afraid of Black taking the c3 pawn.</skip>

**12...Bxc3 13.bxc3 Rxc3 14.axb5**

Taking the pawn, opening the a1 Rook.</skip>

**14...axb5 15.Bd2**

Attacking the Rook, preparing Bb4.</skip>

**15...Rc8 16.d5**

White is much better because King is caught in the center.</skip>

**16...Ne7**

Runs into:</skip>

**17.Ba5**

Winning the Queen or the Rook.</skip>

### (607) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Sidelines 3...Na5 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...Na5**

A rare move, the idea of which is to move the Knight away from the threatened ex­change on c6, and then, by attacking the Bishop on b5, regain the lost tempi</skip>

**4.c3**

Making a square for the Bishop on c2, and getting a Ruy Lopez kind of position.</skip>

**4...a6 5.Ba4**

Preparing Bc2.</skip>

**5...b5 6.Bc2**

Defending e4, preparing d4.</skip>

**6...Bb7 7.d4**

**7...cxd4 8.cxd4**

Keeping the strong center.</skip>

**8...Rc8 9.O-O**

**9...Nf6 10.Nbd2**

Preparing the standard Ruy Lopez route for the Knight: Nbd2-Nf1-Ng3.</skip>

**10...d5**

Seems to be a little bit too early for this.</skip>

**11.e5**

Attacking the Knight and leaving the c3-square ready for our Knight.</skip>

**11...Ne4 12.e6**

A typical positional sacrifice, directed against the f8 Bishop that will remain passive for a while.</skip>

**12...f6 13.Re1**

White is much better because Black can't develop the King-side.</skip>

### (608) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Sidelines 3...Na5 #2

**5...b5 6.Bc2**

**6...Bb7 7.d4**

**7...cxd4 8.cxd4**

Heading to f1.</skip>

White has a nice pawn center, and extra space. 1-0 (48) Caruana,F (2828)-Iturrizaga Bonelli,E (2637) chess.com INT 2019</skip>

### (611) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Sidelines 3...e5 #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e5**

Is an interesting sideline, Black's point is to play Nge7 next, and follow up with the simple King-side Fianchetto.</skip>

**4.O-O Nge7**

Is the original idea of this system.</skip>

**5.c3 g6 6.d4**

The most principled approach.</skip>

**6...cxd4 7.cxd4 exd4**

Black has to play with the temporary isolated pawn on d7, but he is going to play d5 soon, trying to get rid of it.</skip>

**8.Nxd4 Bg7 9.Be3 O-O 10.Nc3 d5**

This is the key position of the variation.</skip>

**11.exd5 Nxd5 12.Nxc6 bxc6 13.Nxd5**

Black was threatening to take our Bishop, so we have to eliminate that Knight.</skip>

**13...cxd5 14.Bc5**

Traps the exchange.</skip>

### (612) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Sidelines 3...e5 #2 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e5**

**4.O-O Nge7**

**5.c3 g6 6.d4**

**6...cxd4 7.cxd4 exd4**

**8.Nxd4 Bg7 9.Be3 O-O 10.Nc3 d5**

**11.exd5 Nxd5 12.Nxc6 bxc6 13.Nxd5**

**13...Qxd5**

Is leading to a forced variation:</skip>

**14.Qxd5 cxd5 15.Bc6 Rb8 16.Bxa7 Rxb2 17.Bc5 Rd8 18.Be7**

White wins the exchange.</skip>

Would drop the Queen.</skip>

### (616) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Sidelines 3...e5 #6 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e5**

**4.O-O Bd6**

Was recently tried by Magnus Carlsen a few times.</skip>

**5.c3 a6 6.Ba4 b5 7.Bc2 Nge7 8.d3**

I like the simple approach of Caruana. We are playing now a Ruy Lopez type of position with the misplaced Bishop on d6.</skip>

**8...O-O 9.Be3 Bb7 10.Nh4**

A strong prophylactic move, keeping an eye on the f5 break.</skip>

**10...Re8 11.Nd2 Ng6 12.Nf5 Bf8 13.Qg4 d6 14.h4**

White has an improved version of the Ruy Lopez and went on to win in: 1-0 (57) Caruana,F (2835)-Carlsen,M (2863) Lichess.org INT 2020</skip>

### (617) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Sidelines 3...e5 #7 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...e5**

**4.O-O Bd6**

**5.c3 a6 6.Ba4 b5 7.Bc2 Nge7 8.d3**

**8...Ng6**

This time Magnus tried to avoid the Nh4 idea that Caruana used in the 8...O-O line.</skip>

**9.Be3 O-O 10.Nbd2 Be7**

Black's set-up is looking kind of normal now but the d5 square is free for the Bishop!</skip>

**11.Bb3**

The Bishops finds the best place from the board.</skip>

**11...d6 12.Bd5 Qe8 13.d4**

White's pieces are better placed, so opening up the position is very logical. White went on to win in: 1-0 (40) Caruana,F (2835)-Carlsen,M (2863) Lichess.org INT 2020</skip>

### (618) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Rossolimo Qc7 Sideline #1 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...Qc7**

A normal move, the idea of which is to prevent the doubling of the pawns on c6.</skip>

**4.O-O**

Getting our King to safety.</skip>

**4...Nf6 5.Re1**

Defending e4.</skip>

**5...Nd4 6.Nxd4**

Taking the Knight, creating doubled pawns for Black.</skip>

**6...cxd4 7.e5**

**7...Nd5 8.c3**

Threatening to take on d4.</skip>

**8...dxc3 9.Nxc3**

Developing the Knight with tempo.</skip>

**9...Nxc3 10.dxc3**

Recapturing the Knight, opening the Bishop.</skip>

**10...e6 11.Qg4**

Developing the Queen, postponing Black from O-O.</skip>

**11...b6 12.Bf4**

Developing the Bishop.</skip>

**12...h5 13.Qg5**

It is hard for Black to activate his Bishops, whilst his pawns on d7 and g7 will become ob­jects of attack.</skip>

### (619) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Rossolimo Qc7 Sideline #2 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...Qc7**

**4.O-O**

**4...Nf6 5.Re1**

**5...e6 6.e5**

**6...Ng4 7.d4**

Opening the position because Blask has an akward Knight on g4.</skip>

**7...cxd4 8.Bxc6**

Eliminating one of the attackers of e5!</skip>

**8...dxc6 9.h3**

Attacking the Knight, and making a luft.</skip>

**9...Nh6 10.Bxh6**

White will have an advantage in development and space.</skip>

### (620) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Rossolimo Qc7 Sideline #3

**5...e6 6.e5**

Stopping Black from going Nd3.</skip>

Developing the Bishop, defending e5.</skip>

### (621) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Rossolimo Qc7 Sideline #4 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...Qc7**

**4.O-O**

**4...Nf6 5.Re1**

**5...e6 6.e5**

**6...Nd5 7.c4**

**7...Nb6 8.Bxc6**

Trading the Bishop for the Knight because on b5, it was getting out of play.</skip>

**8...Qxc6 9.b3**

Defending the pawn.</skip>

**9...d5 10.exd6**

Taking the pawn, not allowing Black to have a strong center.</skip>

**10...Bxd6 11.d4**

Opening up the position.</skip>

**11...O-O 12.dxc5**

Taking the pawn and leaving that square available for one of our pieces.</skip>

**12...Bxc5 13.Ba3**

Trading the strong Bishop!</skip>

**13...f6 14.Nd4**

Attacking the Queen.</skip>

**14...Bxd4 15.Qxd4 e5 16.Qe3**

White is better because the Knight will land on d5 sooner or later.</skip>

### (622) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Rossolimo Qc7 Sideline #5 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...Qc7**

**4.O-O**

**4...Nf6 5.Re1**

**5...e6 6.e5**

**6...Nd5 7.c4**

**7...Nb6 8.Bxc6**

**8...bxc6 9.d3**

Defending c4.</skip>

**9...d5 10.b3**

Keeping the structure solid, Black has a weak pawn on c5.</skip>

**10...Be7 11.Nc3**

Developing the Knight and getting ready to jump to e4 if they advance the d-pawn.</skip>

**11...O-O 12.Bf4**

Developing the Bishop, overprotecting e5.</skip>

**12...Rd8 13.Qc1**

Sidestepping the distant duel between Rook and Queen.</skip>

**13...Rd7 14.Ne2**

Preparing to join the King-side attack.</skip>

**14...a5 15.a4**

Stopping any counterplay on the Queen-side.</skip>

**15...Qb8 16.Qe3**

White has free play on the King-side, while Black can only sit and wait for a mistake.</skip>

### (623) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Rossolimo Qc7 Sideline #6 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...Qc7**

**4.O-O**

**4...e6 5.c3**

Preparing d4.</skip>

**5...Nf6 6.Re1**

Defending e4.</skip>

**6...d5 7.e5**

**7...Nd7 8.d4**

We have reached a very favorable ver­sion of the French Defense for White: he has seized space, successfully positioned his pieces and solidly defended the key pawn on d4. Note that in such a structure, the Black Queen would be better placed on b6, from where it exerts pressure on the d4 pawn, on c7, it does not have any particular prospects.</skip>

Developing the Bishop.</skip>

### (630) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Rossolimo 3...Qc7 4.O-O a6 #2 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...Qc7**

**4.O-O**

**4...a6 5.Bxc6**

**5...dxc6 6.a4**

**6...a5 7.d4**

Opening up the position because Black has no development!</skip>

**7...cxd4 8.Qxd4**

Developing the Queen.</skip>

**8...Bg4 9.Qe5**

Going for a better endgame.</skip>

**9...Qxe5 10.Nxe5**

Attacking the Bishop.</skip>

**10...Be2 11.Re1**

Attacking the Bishop.</skip>

**11...Ba6 12.Bd2**

White finds the thread, with which he will disrupt the whole complex of black pawns on the Queen-side. The bad posi­tion of the Bishop on a6 is already start­ing to tell.</skip>

**12...f6 13.Nf3**

Retreating the Knight. We keep the a5 pawn under attack.</skip>

**13...b6 14.e5**

Stopping Black from playing e5.</skip>

**14...O-O-O 15.b4**

Attacking the Queen-side.</skip>

**15...axb4 16.Bxb4 c5 17.Bc3**

Controlling the center.</skip>

**17...Nh6 18.Nbd2**

White is better because Black's Bishop is stuck on f8, and White can also attack the b6 pawn.</skip>

### (631) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Rossolimo 3...Qc7 4.O-O a6 #3 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...Qc7**

**4.O-O**

**4...a6 5.Bxc6**

**5...bxc6**

Is too slow. White quietly pre­pares a pawn offensive in the centre and obtains comfortable play.</skip>

**6.Re1**

Defending the e4 pawn, preparing c3-d4.</skip>

**6...e5 7.c3**

Preparing to break with d4.</skip>

**7...d6 8.d4**

**8...cxd4 9.cxd4**

Keeping a solid center.</skip>

**9...Bg4 10.dxe5**

Taking the pawn, creating a weakness on c6.</skip>

**10...dxe5**

Notice how the c6 pawn became isolated.</skip>

**11.h3 Bxf3 12.Qxf3**

Recapturing the piece, keeping a healthy pawn structure.</skip>

**12...Nf6 13.Be3**

As a result of all the exchanges in the centre, Black has a permanent weakness on c6.</skip>

**13...Be7 14.Nd2**

Developing the Knight, preparing Rc1.</skip>

**14...O-O 15.Rec1**

White is ready to double the Rooks on the c-file, and press on the isolated pawn. 1-0 (40) Komliakov,V (2463)-Unni,C (2205) ICC INT 2000</skip>

Attacking the Queen.</skip>

Attacking the Queen.</skip>

### (633) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Rossolimo 3...Qc7 4.O-O a6 #5

Is inviting our typical sacrifice!</skip>

Weakening the Queen-side at first.</skip>

The point!We are attacking Black in the center because he is underdeveloped.</skip>

**11...exd5 12.exd5+**

**12...Be7 13.Qg4**

### (634) 12. Rossolimo Sidelines - Rossolimo 3...Qc7 4.O-O a6 #6 

**1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5**

**3...Qc7**

**4.O-O**

**4...a6 5.Bxc6**

**5...Qxc6 6.d4**

**6...cxd4 7.Nxd4**

**7...Qc7 8.Nc3**

**8...e6 9.Re1**

**9...d6 10.Bf4**

White's idea is to use his advantage in de­velopment to attack the pawn on d6 and force the Hedgehog structure d6-e6 to commit itself prematurely.</skip>

**10...e5**

Looks tempting to fork White, but is it going to be enough to balance the game?</skip>

**11.Nd5**

And the answer is: No! Because White has a strong initiative in the center.</skip>

**11...Qd8 12.Be3**

Preparing to recapture on d4 with the B.</skip>

**12...exd4**

Wins the piece for now, but compensation is on its way due to White's lead in development.</skip>

**13.Bxd4**

The threat of going Bb6 is too strong.</skip>

**13...Be6 14.Bb6**

Attacking the Queen, and preparing Nc7.</skip>

**14...Qc8 15.Nc7+**

Forking the King and the Rook.</skip>

**15...Ke7 16.e5**

Threatening to mate on d6, and opening up the position.</skip>

**16...dxe5 17.Rxe5**

White is down a piece at the moment, but he is fully developed and ready to mate the enemy King.</skip>

Attacking the Queen, and threatening Nc7, Black had to give up the Queen.</skip>

**14...cxd5 15.Bxd8 Kxd8 16.exd5**

White is up a Queen and a pawn for three minor pieces.Normally this would not be that clear, but in our case, Black's pieces are passive, and the King is very weak on d8, so White will push the Queen-side pawns with a much better position. 1-0 (32) Maze,S (2530)-Roy Chowdhury,S (2420) Montpellier 2007</skip>
